    <meta name="title" content="Hybrid Genomic Analysis of Salmonella enterica Serovar Enteritidis SE3 Isolated from Polluted Soil in Brazil">
    <meta name="description" content="In Brazil, Salmonella enterica serovar Enteritidis is a significant health threat. Salmonella&nbsp;enterica serovar Enteritidis SE3 was isolated from soil at the Subaé River in Santo Amaro, Brazil, a region contaminated with heavy metals and organic waste. Illumina HiSeq and Oxford Nanopore Technologies MinION sequencing were used for de novo hybrid assembly of the Salmonella SE3 genome. This approach yielded 10 contigs with 99.98% identity with S. enterica serovar Enteritidis OLF-SE2-98984-6. Twelve Salmonella pathogenic islands, multiple virulence genes, multiple antimicrobial gene resistance genes, seven phage defense systems, seven prophages and a heavy metal resistance gene were encoded in the genome. Pangenome analysis of the S. enterica clade, including Salmonella SE3, revealed an open pangenome, with a core genome of 2137 genes. Our study showed the effectiveness of a hybrid sequence assembly approach for environmental Salmonella genome analysis using HiSeq and MinION data. This approach enabled the identification of key resistance and virulence genes, and these data are important to inform the control of Salmonella and heavy metal pollution in the Santo Amaro region of Brazil.">

<div id="drop-article-label-openaccess" class="f-dropdown medium" data-dropdown-content="" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1">
    <p>
        All articles published by MDPI are made immediately available worldwide under an open access license. No special 
        permission is required to reuse all or part of the article published by MDPI, including figures and tables. For 
        articles published under an open access Creative Common CC BY license, any part of the article may be reused without 
        permission provided that the original article is clearly cited. For more information, please refer to
        <a href="https://www.mdpi.com/openaccess">https://www.mdpi.com/openaccess</a>.
    </p>
</div>

<div id="drop-article-label-feature" class="f-dropdown medium" data-dropdown-content="" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1">
    <p>
        Feature papers represent the most advanced research with significant potential for high impact in the field. A Feature 
        Paper should be a substantial original Article that involves several techniques or approaches, provides an outlook for 
        future research directions and describes possible research applications.
    </p>

    <p>
        Feature papers are submitted upon individual invitation or recommendation by the scientific editors and must receive 
        positive feedback from the reviewers.
    </p>
</div>

<div id="drop-article-label-choice" class="f-dropdown medium" data-dropdown-content="" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1">
    <p>
        Editor’s Choice articles are based on recommendations by the scientific editors of MDPI journals from around the world. 
        Editors select a small number of articles recently published in the journal that they believe will be particularly 
        interesting to readers, or important in the respective research area. The aim is to provide a snapshot of some of the 
        most exciting work published in the various research areas of the journal.

        </p><div style="margin-top: -10px;">
            <div id="drop-article-label-choice-journal-link" style="display: none; margin-top: -10px; padding-top: 10px;">
            </div>
        </div>
    <p></p>
</div>

<div id="drop-article-label-resubmission" class="f-dropdown medium" data-dropdown-content="" aria-hidden="true" tabindex="-1">
    <p>
        Original Submission Date Received: <span id="drop-article-label-resubmission-date"></span>.
    </p>
</div>

<div class="html-dynamic">

            
                        
        
        <section>
            <div class="art-abstract art-abstract-new in-tab hypothesis_container">
                                    <p>
                        </p><div><section class="html-abstract" id="html-abstract">

        <h2 id="html-abstract-title">Abstract</h2><b>:</b>
        <div class="html-p">In Brazil, <span class="html-italic">Salmonella enterica</span> serovar Enteritidis is a significant health threat. <span class="html-italic">Salmonella&nbsp;enterica</span> serovar Enteritidis SE3 was isolated from soil at the Subaé River in Santo Amaro, Brazil, a region contaminated with heavy metals and organic waste. Illumina HiSeq and Oxford Nanopore Technologies MinION sequencing were used for de novo hybrid assembly of the <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 genome. This approach yielded 10 contigs with 99.98% identity with <span class="html-italic">S. enterica</span> serovar Enteritidis OLF-SE2-98984-6. Twelve <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> pathogenic islands, multiple virulence genes, multiple antimicrobial gene resistance genes, seven phage defense systems, seven prophages and a heavy metal resistance gene were encoded in the genome. Pangenome analysis of the <span class="html-italic">S. enterica</span> clade, including <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3, revealed an open pangenome, with a core genome of 2137 genes. Our study showed the effectiveness of a hybrid sequence assembly approach for environmental <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> genome analysis using HiSeq and MinION data. This approach enabled the identification of key resistance and virulence genes, and these data are important to inform the control of <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> and heavy metal pollution in the Santo Amaro region of Brazil.</div>


  </section>

  <div id="html-keywords">
    <div class="html-gwd-group"><div id="html-keywords-title">Keywords: </div><a href="/search?q=whole+genome+sequencing">whole genome sequencing</a>; <a href="/search?q=Salmonella"><span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span></a>; <a href="/search?q=hybrid+sequence+assembly">hybrid sequence assembly</a>; <a href="/search?q=heavy+metal">heavy metal</a>; <a href="/search?q=antimicrobial+resistance">antimicrobial resistance</a></div>

<div class="html-body">
    <section id="sec1-microorganisms-11-00111" type="intro"><h2 data-nested="1">  1. Introduction</h2><div class="html-p">Salmonellosis, one of the primary causes of foodborne infections resulting from gram-negative enteropathogenic bacteria <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> spp., is a global threat to human health [<a href="#B1-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">1</a>]. Typhoidal <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> causes enteric fever in humans, whereas non-typhoidal <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> (NTS) results in acute/chronic gastroenteritis. Annually, it is estimated that NTS is responsible for ~93.8 million infections and ~155,000 deaths [<a href="#B2-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">2</a>]. </div><div class="html-p">NTS infections cause diarrhoea and a non-specific febrile illness that is clinically indistinguishable from other febrile illnesses [<a href="#B3-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">3</a>]. <span class="html-italic">Salmonella enterica</span> subspecies <span class="html-italic">enterica</span> has more than 2600 serovars according to unique somatic (O) and flagellar (H) antigenic formulae [<a href="#B4-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">4</a>,<a href="#B5-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">5</a>]. <span class="html-italic">S. enterica</span> sv. Typhimurium and <span class="html-italic">S. enterica</span> sv. Enteritidis are the main pathogens responsible for causing gastroenteritis in humans [<a href="#B6-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">6</a>,<a href="#B7-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">7</a>].</div><div class="html-p">To prevent the occurrence of the main <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> serovars worldwide, several prevention and control measures are adopted in farms and food processing industries. In Brazil, <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> infection of flocks and transmission to poultry-derived food is a major transmission route for the pathogen. <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> is routinely managed on Brazilian farms by poultry vaccination and laboratory testing (Available online: <a href="https://www.gov.br/agricultura/pt-br/assuntos/sanidade-animal-e-vegetal/saude-animal/programas-desaude-animal/pnsa/2003_78.INconsolidada.pdf" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://www.gov.br/agricultura/pt-br/assuntos/sanidade-animal-e-vegetal/saude-animal/programas-desaude-animal/pnsa/2003_78.INconsolidada.pdf</a> (accessed on 18 December 2022)). However, despite these measures several poultry diseases and foodborne <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> outbreaks have been reported in Brazil in recent decades [<a href="#B8-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">8</a>].</div><div class="html-p">Whole-genome sequencing (WGS) is useful in foodborne outbreak investigations and pathogen surveillance [<a href="#B9-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">9</a>]. Illumina short-read sequencing technology has proven to be robust for characterizing pathogens of clinical care [<a href="#B10-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">10</a>], but it is unable to resolve repetitive and GC-rich regions, thus producing unresolvable regions in the underlying genome assembly [<a href="#B11-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">11</a>]. These unresolved regions impede completion of a whole-genome structure, which is crucial to determine if some genes are co-regulated or co-transmissible, and if they are located on the chromosome or plasmids [<a href="#B12-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">12</a>]. Furthermore, the bias to identify key virulence genes during an outbreak investigation can also have negative impacts on public health assessment. </div><div class="html-p">Nanopore sequencing technology can generate long reads to facilitate the completion of bacterial genome assemblies but can lack sequencing depth in some repetitive regions [<a href="#B13-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">13</a>]. However, nanopore’s long reads can span wide repetitive regions and help solve GC-rich regions, making it useful for resolving full-length genome sequences [<a href="#B14-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">14</a>]. Nanopore sequencing technology exhibits lower read accuracy than Illumina sequencing which can produce systematic errors, as a result, it has only usually been applied as a complement to short-read sequencing for bacterial genome assembly [<a href="#B15-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">15</a>]. Since the release of the MinION platform by Oxford Nanopore Technologies, nanopore chemistry, base-calling, and bioinformatic tools have been steadily improving and are now more able to produce accurate bacterial genome sequences independent of other sequencing technologies [<a href="#B16-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">16</a>].</div><div class="html-p">The combination of both short reads for base-calling accuracy and long reads for structural integrity has recently been developed as a hybrid assembly approach to close whole-genome assemblies, such as those found in the Unicycler and SPAdes pipelines [<a href="#B17-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">17</a>,<a href="#B18-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">18</a>]. Unicycler was specifically developed for hybrid assembly of bacterial genomes [<a href="#B18-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">18</a>]. Unicycler generates a short-read assembly graph and then uses long-reads to build bridges to resolve all repeats in the genome, performs multiple rounds of short-read polishing and finally, it produces a complete genome assembly [<a href="#B14-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">14</a>].</div><div class="html-p">In this study, a hybrid genome assembly approach using MinION and HiSeq sequencing data was used to improve the assembly parameters and gene completeness, identification of virulence and antimicrobial resistance genes (ARG), genome phylogeny and pangenome in <span class="html-italic">Salmonella enterica</span> var. Enteritidis SE3 isolated from soil at the Subaé river in Santo Amaro, Brazil, a river polluted with organic waste and heavy metals.</div></section><section id="sec2-microorganisms-11-00111" type=""><h2 data-nested="1">  2. Materials and Methods</h2><div class="html-p">Environmental soil samples were obtained from the Subaé river basin in Santo Amaro, Salvador de Bahia, Brazil. Approximately 100 g of soil sample was collected from river soil (12°31′46.77″ S 38°44′1.24″ W). The sample was transported in a refrigerated box (4–8 °C) to the laboratory where the analyses were undertaken immediately.</div><section id="sec2dot1-microorganisms-11-00111" type=""><h4 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  2.1. Salmonella Isolation</h4><div class="html-p"><span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> was isolated according to the US Food and Drug Administration Bacteriological Analytical Manual (<a href="https://www.fda.gov/media/79991/download" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://www.fda.gov/media/79991/download</a> (accessed on 18 December 2022)). Briefly, 10 g or 10 mL of samples of each sample were pre-enriched in 100 mL lactose broth (supplier), at 37 °C for 24 h, 0.1 mL of pre-enriched culture was transferred to 10 mL enriched in Tetrathionate (TT) broth (HIMEDIA, Kennett Square, PA, USA) and incubated at 41 °C for 24 h. Broth cultures from the selective enrichment broth were plated on Xylose-Lysine-Desoxycholate (XLD) agar (HIMEDIA, Kennett Square, PA, USA), Bismuth sulfite agar (Acumedia Manufacters Inc., San Bernardino, CA, USA) and Salmonella Shigella (SS) agar (HIMEDIA, PA, Kennett Square, USA). Colonies characteristic of <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> having a slightly transparent zone of reddish color and a black center for XLD, gray or brown-black colonies with or without metallic sheen for Bismuth Sulfite Agar, and beige colonies with black centers for SS agar were identified and picked. Then, the isolates were tested biochemically using the Triple Sugar Iron (TSI) test. <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> strains were confirmed when they showed good to excellent growth, pink colonies with black centers were detected, and the agar was red [<a href="#B19-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">19</a>]. </div></section><section id="sec2dot2-microorganisms-11-00111" type=""><h4 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  2.2. DNA Isolation</h4><div class="html-p">For bacteria, a single colony was enriched in 5 mL Luria Bertani (LB) broth, and 15 mL of enrichment broth was transferred to a centrifuge tube and centrifuged at 4000 rpm for 10 min. DNA from <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> strains was extracted and purified using the E.Z.N.A. Bacterial DNA Mini Kit (Omega Biotek, Norcross, GA, USA) following the instructions provided by the manufacturer. For phages, a crude lysate was centrifuged the lysate as described. DNA isolation from phages was carried out using the E.Z.N.A. Viral DNA Mini Isolation Kit (Omega Biotek, Norcross, GA, USA) following the instructions provided by the manufacturer. The quality and concentration of the bacteria and phage DNA was evaluated by Qubit Fluorometric Quantification (ThermoFisher Scientific, Waltham, MA, USA) and gel electrophoresis (1% of agarose gel, 80 V for 45 min in 1x TAE Buffer).</div></section><section id="sec2dot3-microorganisms-11-00111" type=""><h4 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  2.3. Amplification of 16S rRNA Gene</h4><div class="html-p">PCR amplification was performed using a Veriti<sup>TM</sup> 96-Well Thermal Cycler (Applied Biosystems, Foster City, CA, USA), 16S gene Amplification PCR for the amplification of the 16S rRNA gene was carried out using universal primers 27F (5′-AGAGTTTGATCATGGCTCAG-3′) as forward and 1492R (5′-GGTTACCTTGTTACGACTT-3′) as a reverse primer [<a href="#B20-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">20</a>]. Approximately 10–100 ng of template was added to a reaction mix containing 10 μL Master Mix 2x (Qiagen, Germantown, MD, USA), 1 μL primer 27 F (10 μM), 1 μL primer 1492R (10 μM), and 1 μL reverse primer (10 μM). PCR was performed with the following cycling conditions: initial denaturation at 95 °C for 10 min, 35 cycles of denaturation at 95 °C for 1 min, annealing from 50 °C to 60 °C for 1 min, and extension at 72 °C for 1 min. A final extension was performed at 72 °C for 7 min. PCR products were visualized using GelRed (Biotium, San Francisco, CA, USA) on a 2% agarose gel which had been run at 80 V for 30 min. The separated PCR products were visualized under UV light and photographed.</div></section><section id="sec2dot4-microorganisms-11-00111" type=""><h4 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  2.4. 16S rRNA Gene Sequencing and Phylogenetic Analysis</h4><div class="html-p">The amplified 16S rRNA PCR products were purified and sequenced at Macrogen (Seoul, Republic of Korea) using the ABI 3100 sequencer with Big Dye Terminator Kit v.3.1. The same 16S rRNA primer sequences used for PCR were used for sequencing. The sequences were assembled and trimmed using Geneious Prime and submitted to the Greengenes database (<a href="https://rnacentral.org/expert-database/greengenes" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://rnacentral.org/expert-database/greengenes</a>, accessed on 18 December 2022). The sequences of this study and reference sequences were aligned with Clustal W, and the evolutionary history was inferred using the Neighbor Joining Method [<a href="#B21-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">21</a>] and the percentage of replicate trees in which the associated taxa clustered together in the bootstrap test (1000 replicates). There were a total of 1552 positions in the final dataset. Evolutionary analyses were conducted in MEGA X [<a href="#B21-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">21</a>].</div></section><section id="sec2dot5-microorganisms-11-00111" type=""><h4 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  2.5. Whole Genome Sequencing (WGS) by MinION and Illumina</h4><div class="html-p">Nanopore WGS sequencing was carried out at the Molecular and Computational Biology of Fungi Laboratory, Federal University of Minas Gerais (UFMG). The DNA library was prepared with ligation Sequencing kit (SQKRAD004, Oxford Nanopore Technologies, Oxford, UK) according to the manufacturer’s instructions. Libraries were sequenced with qualified FLO-MIN106 flow cells (the initial bias voltage was −210 mV and the active pores number around 516) for 36 h (basecalling function was used, the reads sequences was filtered using a min_score = 9) on a MinION (Oxford Nanopore Technologies, Oxford, UK) [<a href="#B22-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">22</a>].</div><div class="html-p">The quality of the sequencing was verified through the FastQC v0.11.9 program (<a href="https://github.com/s-andrews/FastQC" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://github.com/s-andrews/FastQC</a>, accessed on 18 December 2022). The Porechop v0.2.4 program [<a href="#B18-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">18</a>] was used for the detection and elimination of the adapters, as well as for the demultiplexing of the Nanopore reads. Possible sequencing errors were treated with the Canu v2.1.1 monitor correction module [<a href="#B23-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">23</a>]. The de novo assembly based on de Bruijn graphs of corrected sequences was carried out through the Flye v2.8.3 [<a href="#B24-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">24</a>]. The contigs obtained using de novo assembly were subjected to a polishing (correction of raw contigs) with the Racon v1.4.22 program [<a href="#B25-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">25</a>], which took the read mappings made with BWA v0.7.17 [<a href="#B26-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">26</a>]. </div><div class="html-p">The Illumina sequencing library was prepared from genomic DNA [1 µg] using the NEBNext Fast DNA Fragmentation and Library Preparation Kit (New England Biolabs, Ipswich, MA, USA) following the manufacturer’s recommendations. The library quality was assessed using the Agilent 2100 Bioanalyzer equipment, and the paired-end DNA sequencing was carried out in the Illumina HiSeq 2500 platform. After sequencing, the raw read quality was assessed using the FastQC v0.11.5 software (<a href="https://github.com/s-andrews/FastQC" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://github.com/s-andrews/FastQC</a>, accessed on 15 January 2020). </div></section><section id="sec2dot6-microorganisms-11-00111" type=""><h4 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  2.6. Hybrid Genome Sequence Assembly</h4><div class="html-p">MinION long-reads were assembled using the Racon pipeline with default parameters [<a href="#B24-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">24</a>] while Illumina short reads were assembled using the (i) SPAdes version: 3.15.3 [<a href="#B27-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">27</a>], (ii) Unicycler [<a href="#B18-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">18</a>] and (iii) Edena [<a href="#B28-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">28</a>] software with default parameters. Hybrid assemblies using Illumina and MinION reads were performed using the software (i) MaSuRCA [<a href="#B29-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">29</a>], and (ii) Unicycler. Genome quality and completeness for each assembly were evaluated using QUAST v4.6.0 [<a href="#B30-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">30</a>], and BUSCO v4 (Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs) [<a href="#B31-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">31</a>]. BUSCO analyses were performed using the database bacteria obd_10.</div></section><section id="sec2dot7-microorganisms-11-00111" type=""><h4 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  2.7. Serotype Identification</h4><div class="html-p">The identification of the serotype was carried out from the de novo contigs, using the SeqSero2 v1.2.1 program [<a href="#B32-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">32</a>].</div></section><section id="sec2dot8-microorganisms-11-00111" type=""><h4 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  2.8. Gene Annotation</h4><div class="html-p">The annotation of genes for both the bacterial and plasmid genomes was performed through the predictor, based on hidden Markov models, Prokka v1.14.6 [<a href="#B33-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">33</a>].</div></section><section id="sec2dot9-microorganisms-11-00111" type=""><h4 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  2.9. Genome Similarity Assessment</h4><div class="html-p"><span class="html-italic">Salmonella enterica</span> genomes (16,638) were downloaded from the NCBI Genbank database in July 2022. Genomes with more than 500 contigs were removed, and contigs smaller than 500 bp were removed from the remaining genomes. Genome quality was evaluated with CheckM v.1.0.13 [<a href="#B34-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">34</a>], using completeness and contamination score of ≥90% and ≤10%, respectively. Genome-distance estimation of genomes was performed with Mash v.2.2.1 [<a href="#B35-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">35</a>]. Near-identical redundant genomes were removed using in-house scripts to cluster genomes assemblies sharing pairwise Mash distances less than 0.005 (~99.95% average nucleotide identity (ANI)) and cluster representatives were chosen based on assembly N50. Further, the genome dataset was taxonomically verified using the Genome Taxonomy Database (GTDB). To investigate the genomic relatedness of the <span class="html-italic">S. enterica</span> SE3 strain and Genbank genomes, a genome-distance tree was built using a combination-distance matrix of Mash and ANI values, computed with Mash v.2.2.1 and fastANI [<a href="#B36-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">36</a>], respectively. </div></section><section id="sec2dot10-microorganisms-11-00111" type=""><h4 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  2.10. Pangenome Analysis</h4><div class="html-p">The <span class="html-italic">S. enterica</span> pangenome analysis was performed with Roary v.3.6, using 90% identity threshold to determine gene clusters [<a href="#B37-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">37</a>]. The Heaps law model was used to estimate the pangenome openness. Core genes (present in up to 95% of the genomes) were aligned with MAFFT v.7.394 [<a href="#B38-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">38</a>]. SNPs were extracted from the core-genome alignment using SNP-sites v.2.3.3 [<a href="#B39-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">39</a>]. The phylogenetic tree was constructed using IQ-TREE [<a href="#B40-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">40</a>], with ascertainment bias correction under the model GTR+ASC, and bootstrap support was performed using 1000 replicates. The resulting phylogenetic tree was visualized and rendered with iTOL v4 [<a href="#B41-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">41</a>].  </div></section><section id="sec2dot11-microorganisms-11-00111" type=""><h4 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  2.11. Mobile Genetic Element Identification and Annotation</h4><div class="html-p">Genomic islands were identified using Island Viewer software (<a href="http://www.pathogenomics.sfu.ca/islandviewer/upload/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">www.pathogenomics.sfu.ca/islandviewer/upload/</a> (accessed on 18 December 2022).) [<a href="#B42-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">42</a>], virulomes were detected using VFanalyser/VFDB (<a href="http://www.mgc.ac.cn/cgi-bin/VFs/v5/main.cgi" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">www.mgc.ac.cn/cgi-bin/VFs/v5/main.cgi</a> (accessed on 18 December 2022)) [<a href="#B43-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">43</a>], resistomes were identified using ResFinder-4.1 (<a href="https://cge.cbs.dtu.dk//cgi-bin/webface.fcgi?jobid=61358037000023BC9E7A4C58" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://cge.cbs.dtu.dk//cgi-bin/webface.fcgi?jobid=61358037000023BC9E7A4C58</a> (accessed on 18 December 2022)) [<a href="#B44-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">44</a>], and CARD (<a href="https://card.mcmaster.ca/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://card.mcmaster.ca/</a> (accessed on 18 December 2022)) [<a href="#B45-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">45</a>], Prophages were identified using Phaster (<a href="http://www.phaster.ca" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">www.phaster.ca</a> (accessed on 18 December 2022)) [<a href="#B46-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">46</a>], phage defense systems were detected using PADLOC (<a href="https://padloc.otago.ac.nz/padloc/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://padloc.otago.ac.nz/padloc/</a> (accessed on 18 December 2022)) [<a href="#B47-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">47</a>] and DefenseFinder (<a href="https://defense-finder.mdmparis-lab.com/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://defense-finder.mdmparis-lab.com/</a> (accessed on 18 December 2022)) [<a href="#B48-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">48</a>]. SPIFinder 2.0 was used to detect Pathogenic Islands (<a href="https://cge.cbs.dtu.dk/services/SPIFinder/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://cge.cbs.dtu.dk/services/SPIFinder/</a> (accessed on 18 December 2022)) [<a href="#B49-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">49</a>]. BRIG was used to draw the chromosomal <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> genomes (<a href="http://brig.sourceforge.net/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">http://brig.sourceforge.net/</a> (accessed on 18 December 2022)) [<a href="#B50-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">50</a>].</div></section></section><section id="sec3-microorganisms-11-00111" type="results"><h2 data-nested="1">  3. Results</h2><section id="sec3dot1-microorganisms-11-00111" type=""><h4 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  3.1. Salmonella Isolation and Characterization</h4><div class="html-p">Presumptive <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> were isolated from soil at the Subaé River using <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> selective growth media. Isolates showed typical <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> characteristics: on XLD colonies had a slightly transparent zone of reddish color and a black center, on Bismuth Sulfite Agar there were gray or brown-black colonies with or without metallic sheen and in SS agar the colonies were beige with black centers. In biochemical tests, good growth was seen in TSI, with acid and gas reactions at depth, an alkaline surface (red) and presence of H<sub>2</sub>S.</div></section><section id="sec3dot2-microorganisms-11-00111" type=""><h4 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  3.2. Analysis of 16S rRNA</h4><div class="html-p">The presumptive <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> isolates were confirmed by 16S rRNA PCR amplification [<a href="#B50-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">50</a>,<a href="#B51-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">51</a>] and sequencing, followed by a sequence query of the Greengenes database. Analysis of the queries returned coverage of 100% and an E value of 0, with 99.91% identity to the same sequence, <span class="html-italic">Salmonella enterica</span> serovar Enteritidis (ID: MT621365.1).</div></section><section id="sec3dot3-microorganisms-11-00111" type=""><h4 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  3.3. Whole Genome Sequencing of Salmonella Isolate SE3</h4><div class="html-p">One of the <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> Enteritidis isolates, designated SE3, was sequenced by Illumina HiSeq and Oxford Nanopore MinION technologies. The number of reads from HiSeq sequencing was 15,997,283 and the number of reads from MinION sequencing was 13,326, after preprocessing. The MinION long reads had an average size of 5.1 kb, and the longest read was 28.8 kb (<a href="#microorganisms-11-00111-t001" class="html-table">Table 1</a>).</div></section><section id="sec3dot4-microorganisms-11-00111" type=""><h4 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  3.4. Genome Assembly</h4><div class="html-p">Six whole genome sequence assembly strategies, including hybrid and non-hybrid, were tested on the HiSeq and MinION sequencing data from <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 (<a href="#microorganisms-11-00111-t002" class="html-table">Table 2</a>). For Illumina HiSeq assembly, Unicycler had the best performance with 31 contigs, a total length of 4,683,367 bp, largest contig of 1,262,086 bp and N50 of 478.501 bp (<a href="#microorganisms-11-00111-t002" class="html-table">Table 2</a>). The Unicycler hybrid assembly had the best performance for genome assembly overall, with 10 contigs, total length of 4,713,463 bp, largest contig of 519,108 bp and N50 of 2,750,500 bp (<a href="#microorganisms-11-00111-t002" class="html-table">Table 2</a>) (<a href="#microorganisms-11-00111-f001" class="html-fig">Figure 1</a>). When measuring genome completeness, Unicycler HiSeq and Unicycler hybrid assembly had the same result, with 98.4 % of the orthologous (complete) genes searched, 99.4 % were single-copy genes, 1.6 % genes were not identified or missing, and there were no identified single and fragmented genes (<a href="#microorganisms-11-00111-t003" class="html-table">Table 3</a>).</div></section><section id="sec3dot5-microorganisms-11-00111" type=""><h4 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  3.5. Completeness of the Genome Annotation</h4><div class="html-p">The genome of <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 was annotated using Prokka and rRNA, tRNA and gene coding sequences were successfully identified (<a href="#microorganisms-11-00111-t004" class="html-table">Table 4</a> and <a href="#app1-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-app">Table S1</a>). <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 showed ~99.9% ANI with <span class="html-italic">Salmonella enterica</span> subsp. <span class="html-italic">enterica</span> serovar Enteritidis OLF-SE2-98984-6.</div></section><section id="sec3dot6-microorganisms-11-00111" type=""><h4 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  3.6. Genomic Relatedness of Salmonella SE3</h4><div class="html-p">Available <span class="html-italic">S. enterica</span> genomes in the GenBank database (<span class="html-italic">n</span> = 16,638, July 2022) were downloaded but after filtering for CheckM quality, removing highly fragmented and near-identical redundant genomes (see methods for details), the remaining dataset was 1598 genomes. Further genomic identity analysis with a combined matrix of all Mash and fastANI pairwise distances between the genomes identified a further 159 genomes with incorrect taxonomic assignment which were excluded. The distance tree built with the combined matrix showed that the <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 genome was located within the properly classified cluster of <span class="html-italic">S. enterica</span> genomes (<a href="#microorganisms-11-00111-f002" class="html-fig">Figure 2</a>A). The <span class="html-italic">S. enterica</span> dataset comprised 1439 <span class="html-italic">S. enterica</span> genomes sharing Mash distance values up to 0.03 (~97% fastANI identity) (<a href="#microorganisms-11-00111-f002" class="html-fig">Figure 2</a>B).</div></section><section id="sec3dot7-microorganisms-11-00111" type=""><h4 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  3.7. Pangenome Analysis</h4><div class="html-p">The pangenome of 1439 <span class="html-italic">S. enterica</span> genomes is composed of 74,995 gene clusters, including a core genome (present in at least 95% of the genomes) of 2137 genes. The accessory genome comprises 3390 shell or shared genes (present from 15% to 95% of the genomes) and 69,352 cloud or singletons genes (present in up to 15% of the genomes) (<a href="#microorganisms-11-00111-f003" class="html-fig">Figure 3</a>B). The Heaps Law estimate supports an open pangenome (alpha = 0.52) for <span class="html-italic">S. enterica</span>. (<a href="#microorganisms-11-00111-f003" class="html-fig">Figure 3</a>A), indicating a high genetic diversity, and the capacity of this sympatric species to rapidly acquire exogenous DNA. We also performed a maximum-likelihood phylogenetic reconstruction using 292,004 SNPs extracted from core genes. This analysis revealed that <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 belongs to a monophyletic clade containing 23 <span class="html-italic">S. enterica</span> strains of serovar Enteritidis (<a href="#microorganisms-11-00111-f003" class="html-fig">Figure 3</a>C).</div></section><section id="sec3dot8-microorganisms-11-00111" type=""><h4 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  3.8. Genome Features</h4><section id="sec3dot8dot1-microorganisms-11-00111" type=""><h4 class="" data-nested="3">  3.8.1. Resistome Identification</h4><div class="html-p">Several resistance mechanisms were identified in <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 using the CARD database; resistance to aminoglycosides (alleles of <span class="html-italic">AAC(6’)-Iy, kdpE, baeR</span>), fluoroquinolones (alleles <span class="html-italic">of MdtK, emrB, emrR, sdiA, Escherichia coli acrA, acrB, rsmA, adeF</span>), macrolides (alleles of <span class="html-italic">Klebsiella pneumoniae</span> KpnE, <span class="html-italic">K. pneumoniae</span> KpnF, H-NS, CRP), monobactam (<span class="html-italic">golS</span>), nitroimidazole (<span class="html-italic">msbA</span>), tetracycline (<span class="html-italic">E. coli mdfA</span>), cephalosporin (<span class="html-italic">Haemophilus influenzae</span> PBP3 conferring resistance to beta-lactam antibiotics, <span class="html-italic">E. coli</span> EF-Tu mutants conferring resistance to Pulvomycin, <span class="html-italic">E. coli uhpT</span> with mutation conferring resistance to Fosfomycin, <span class="html-italic">E. coli glpT</span> with mutation conferring resistance to Fode novosfomycin), <a href="#microorganisms-11-00111-f004" class="html-fig">Figure 4</a>.</div><div class="html-p">According to their mechanism of resistance, the genes were classified as antibiotic efflux (<span class="html-italic">golS, baeR, MdtK, K. pneumoniae</span> KpnE, <span class="html-italic">K. pneumoniae KpnF, H-NS, sdiA, mbsA, E. coli mdfA, kdpE, E. coli acrA, acrB, adeF, CRP, rsmA, emrB, emrR</span> and <span class="html-italic">marA</span>), antibiotic inactivation (<span class="html-italic">AAC(6’)-ly</span>), antibiotic target alteration (<span class="html-italic">vanG, bacA, H. influenzae</span> PBP3 conferring resistance to beta-lactam antibiotics, <span class="html-italic">E. coli uhpT </span>with mutation conferring resistance to Fosfomycin, <span class="html-italic">E. coli</span>&nbsp;<span class="html-italic">EF-Tu</span> mutants conferring resistance to Pulvomycin, <span class="html-italic">E. coli glpT</span> with mutation conferring resistance to Fosfomycin, <span class="html-italic">E. coli</span> EF-Tu mutants conferring resistance to Pulvomycin, <span class="html-italic">pmrF, E. coli</span> acrAB-tolC with marR mutations conferring resistance to ciprofloxacin and tetracycline, <span class="html-italic">E. coli soxR</span> with mutation conferring antibiotic resistance and <span class="html-italic">E. coli soxS</span> with mutation conferring antibiotic resistance). Resfinder identified resistance against aminoglycosides: tobramycin (<span class="html-italic">aac(6’)-Iaa</span> (<span class="html-italic">aac(6’)-Iaa</span>_NC_003197) and amikacin (<span class="html-italic">aac(6’)-Iaa</span> (<span class="html-italic">aac(6’)-Iaa</span>_NC_003197).</div></section><section id="sec3dot8dot2-microorganisms-11-00111" type=""><h4 class="" data-nested="3">  3.8.2. Viriome, Genomic Island and Pathogenic Island Identification</h4><div class="html-p">In total, 144 potential virulence genes were identified in <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 using VFanalyser/VFDB, some of the most important identified were <span class="html-italic">invA</span>, <span class="html-italic">sipA</span>, <span class="html-italic">sipB, sipC, fepA, sopA, sopB, sopD, sopE2, pefA, pefB, pefC, pefD</span> and <span class="html-italic">ssaB</span>. Genomic islands were detected using Island Viewer which uses three prediction methods: Integrated, IslandPath-DIMOB and SIGI-HMM. Twelve pathogenic islands were detected (<a href="#microorganisms-11-00111-f004" class="html-fig">Figure 4</a> and <a href="#microorganisms-11-00111-t005" class="html-table">Table 5</a>), and included virulence genes, secretion proteins, resistance genes, bacteriophage sequence regions, transposases and integrases. The gene <span class="html-italic">arsC</span>, encoding Arsenate reductase was identified in a genomic island. The <span class="html-italic">mdtK</span> gene (encoding multidrug resistance protein MdtK) was also identified in the resistome analysis. Virulence genes identified using Island Viewer were very similar to those identified using VFanalyser/VFDB.</div></section></section><section id="sec3dot9-microorganisms-11-00111" type=""><h4 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  3.9. Identification of Antiviral Defense Systems</h4><div class="html-p">Several antiviral defense system virulence genes were identified using PADLOC and DefenseFinder tools (<a href="#microorganisms-11-00111-t006" class="html-table">Table 6</a>). Both tools identified several systems: Cas type IE, CBASS type I, CRISPR array, restriction–modification (RM) RM type I, and RM type III. Similar antiviral systems and proteins were identified by PADLOC, except for AbiU and RM type II (<a href="#microorganisms-11-00111-t006" class="html-table">Table 6</a> and <a href="#microorganisms-11-00111-f004" class="html-fig">Figure 4</a>).</div></section><section id="sec3dot10-microorganisms-11-00111" type=""><h4 class="html-italic" data-nested="2">  3.10. Prophage Identification</h4><div class="html-p">Of the prophages identified in <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 using PHASTER, two regions were intact, five regions were incomplete, and none were questionable (<a href="#microorganisms-11-00111-t007" class="html-table">Table 7</a>). Proteins were identified in the Gisfy and RE-2010 prophages including lysis, terminase, portal protein, protease, coat protein, tail shaft, attachment site, integrase, tail fiber and plate proteins.</div></section></section><section id="sec4-microorganisms-11-00111" type="discussion"><h2 data-nested="1">  4. Discussion</h2><div class="html-p"><span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 was isolated from soil at the Subaé River in Santo Amaro, Brazil, a region contaminated with heavy metals and organic waste. The genome sequence of this isolate was determined using two sequencing technologies and six different bioinformatics strategies. Hybrid assembly showed the lowest number of contigs followed by MinION-alone assembly, with hybrid genome assembly resulting in a genome of 4.73 Mb, which was similar in size to that reported (4.68 Mb) for <span class="html-italic">Salmonella enterica</span> subsp. <span class="html-italic">enterica</span> serovar Enteritidis str. P125109 (NC_011294.1) [<a href="#B52-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">52</a>]. However, the GC content of the assembled genome (52.16%) was more similar to <span class="html-italic">Salmonella enterica</span> subsp. enterica serovar Enteritidis str. P125109 (NC_011294.1) (52.17%) [<a href="#B52-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">52</a>]. HiSeq assemblies have been traditionally considered the “gold standard” because MinION sequencing could introduce high numbers of errors and consequently may interfere with high-quality genome annotations due to reduced accuracy in gene prediction, producing a large number of misannotated genes [<a href="#B53-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">53</a>,<a href="#B54-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">54</a>]. However, the genome completeness of <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 with non-hybrid assembly and hybrid assembly were almost identical.</div><div class="html-p">Phylogenetic analysis of the <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 genome revealed it was located within the properly classified cluster of <span class="html-italic">S. enterica</span>. During taxon analysis we identified 159 genomes with incorrect taxonomic classification, highlighting that it is important to confirm identity prior to undertaking phylogenetic analyses. </div><div class="html-p">The pangenome analysis of <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3, revealed the core genome was composed of 2137 genes and the accessory genome comprised 3390 shell genes and 69,352 cloud genes. This indicates <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 has an open pangenome with a diversity of unique genes. A study by Chand et al. [<a href="#B55-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">55</a>] undertook a comparative genomic analysis of 44 genome sequences, representing 17 serovars of <span class="html-italic">S. enterica</span>, and concluded that the genus <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> displays an open pangenome, comprising a reservoir of 10,775 gene families. Of these 2847 constituted the core gene families, 4657 were dispensable or accessory gene families, and 3271 strain-specific gene families. Park et al. [<a href="#B56-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">56</a>] constructed pangenomes of seven species to elucidate variations in the genetic contents of &gt;27,000 genomes, as in our study, this work showed the pangenome of <span class="html-italic">Salmonella enterica</span> subsp. <span class="html-italic">enterica</span> was open. However, it is important to note that pangenome size is heavily influenced by the properties of the genomes used and variation would likely result in inconsistencies, and secondly, newly described genes are often included which results in open pangenomes [<a href="#B57-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">57</a>].</div><div class="html-p">The antimicrobial resistance gene profile of <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 identified genes potentially involved in resistance to aminoglycosides, fluoroquinolones, macrolides, a monobactam (<span class="html-italic">golS</span>), nitroimidazole (<span class="html-italic">msbA</span>), tetracycline and related drugs (<span class="html-italic">mdfA</span>), and cephalosporins. Other studies of <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> isolates from southern Brazil have also reported tetracycline (<span class="html-italic">mdfA</span>) and aminoglycoside (<span class="html-italic">aac(6’)-Iaa</span>) resistance genes, in addition to other genes such as <span class="html-italic">aac(3)-Iva, aph(3”)-Ib, aph(4)-Ia, aph(6)-Id, tet(34)</span> and <span class="html-italic">tet(A)</span> [<a href="#B57-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">57</a>,<a href="#B58-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">58</a>,<a href="#B59-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">59</a>,<a href="#B60-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">60</a>,<a href="#B61-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">61</a>]. In the United States, additional antibiotic resistance mechanisms in <span class="html-italic">S. enterica</span> have been described [<a href="#B62-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">62</a>], such as resistance to aminoglycosides (<span class="html-italic">aadA, aadB, aacC, aphA, strAB</span>), β-lactams (<span class="html-italic">blaCMY-2, PSE-1</span>, <span class="html-italic">TEM-1</span>), chloramphenicol (<span class="html-italic">cat1, cat2, cmlA, floR</span>), inhibitors of the folate pathway (<span class="html-italic">dfr, sul</span>), and tetracycline (<span class="html-italic">tetA, tetB, tetC, tetD, tetG,</span> and <span class="html-italic">tetR</span>), none of these resistance genes were detected in our study. </div><div class="html-p">Ten <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> pathogenic islands were identified in <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 which is relatively high compared that reported for other <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> isolates. A <span class="html-italic">S. enterica</span> serovar Typhimurium isolate, ms202, from a patient in India possessed six <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> pathogenicity islands: SPI-1, SPI-2, SPI-3, SPI-4, SPI-5, and SPI-11 [<a href="#B63-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">63</a>], but in our work, we did not identify SPI-4. The genes identified in SPI regions had similarity to known transporters, drug targets, and antibiotic-resistance genes, and in a subset of genomic islands, genes that facilitate the horizontal transfer of genes encoding numerous resistance and virulence factors of regions belonging to type III secretion systems (T3SS). Vilela et al. [<a href="#B64-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">64</a>] analyzed six <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> Choleraesuis strains provided by the Brazilian <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> reference laboratory of the Oswaldo Cruz Foundation (FIOCRUZ-RJ), which receives <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> isolates from diverse isolation sources and regions of the country. Pathogenicity islands SPI-1, -2, -3, -4, -5, -9, -13, -14 and CS54 island were detected in five strains and SPI-11 in four strains. The majority of these SPI, with the exception of SPI 4 and SPI 11, were also detected in <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3. SPI-1 and SPI-2 are known to be involved in the invasion of intestinal epithelial cells and survival and replication within phagocytic cells, respectively, through the formation of type 3 secretion systems, SPI-5 is associated with fluid secretion and inflammatory response and SPI-3, -4, -11, -13, -14 and CS54 are associated with <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> survival and adaptation to stresses within macrophages [<a href="#B65-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">65</a>].</div><div class="html-p">In total, 144 potential virulence genes were identified in <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3. Some of these virulence genes are also found in other serovars of <span class="html-italic">Salmonella.</span> Borah et al. [<a href="#B66-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">66</a>] investigated virulence genes in 88 <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> isolates recovered from humans and different species of animals. Among the 88 isolates, some virulence genes such <span class="html-italic">invA</span>, <span class="html-italic">sipA</span>, <span class="html-italic">sipB</span> and <span class="html-italic">sipC</span> were detected irrespective of the serovar, and these were also detected in <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3. <span class="html-italic">fepA</span> was also present in a high percentage (64.7%) of isolates belonging to <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> serovars Enteritidis, Weltervreden, Typhi, Newport, Litchfield, Idikan and Typhimurium, as well as <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 and. Other virulence genes were present in varying percentages among the <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> serovars studied by Borah et al. [<a href="#B66-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">66</a>] such as <span class="html-italic">sopB</span> (86.36%), <span class="html-italic">sopE2</span> (62.5%), <span class="html-italic">pefA</span> (79.54%) and <span class="html-italic">sefC</span> (51.14%); of these genes only <span class="html-italic">sefC</span> was not detected in <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3. The virulence genes identified in <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 are involved in several different processes, such as the <span class="html-italic">invA</span> gene usually codes for a protein in the inner bacterial membrane that is responsible for the invasion of intestinal cells of the host [<a href="#B67-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">67</a>,<a href="#B68-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">68</a>]. The <span class="html-italic">fepA</span> gene encodes outer membrane receptor protein FepA, which participates in iron transport and plays a role in infection colonization in <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> [<a href="#B32-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">32</a>]. T3SS-1 secretes proteins, termed effectors, across the inner and outer membranes of the bacterial cell. Some of the secreted effectors, including SipA, SipB and SipC are encoded by genes located on SPI1. The remaining effectors, including SopA, SopB, SopD, SopE and SopE2 are encoded by genes that are scattered around the <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 chromosome. Upon secretion the SipB, SipC, and SipD proteins are thought to form a complex in the eukaryotic membrane that is required for translocation of the remaining effectors into the host cell cytoplasm [<a href="#B69-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">69</a>]. PefA is encoded by <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 and is the plasmid-encoded fimbrial major subunit antigen of <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> Typhimurium [<a href="#B70-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">70</a>]. <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> plasmid-encoded fimbrae have been found to mediate adhesion to mouse intestinal epithelium [<a href="#B71-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">71</a>].</div><div class="html-p">The gene <span class="html-italic">arsC,</span> encoding arsenate reductase, was found in the genome of <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3. Arsenate reductase is essential for arsenate resistance and transforms arsenate into arsenite which is extruded from the cell [<a href="#B72-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">72</a>,<a href="#B73-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">73</a>]. This is of interest as <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 was isolated from the soil of Subaé River where heavy metal concentrations were above reference values [<a href="#B74-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">74</a>]. In addition, mussels (<span class="html-italic">Mytella charruana</span>) gathered from the same region also contained lead, arsenic and cadmium in concentrations above reference values [<a href="#B75-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">75</a>]. Carvalho et al. [<a href="#B75-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">75</a>] also determined the quality of soils in 39 households from nearby Santo Amaro City, and the Residential Investigation Value (RIV) was exceeded by Lead (23.1% of the samples), Cadmium (7.7%), Nickel (2.6%), Zinc (25.6%), Arsenic (2.6%), and Antimony (7.7%).</div><div class="html-p">Several virus defence systems were detected in <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3, including CRISPR-Cas type IE, CBASS type I, and RM type I and III systems. Similar antiviral systems and subtypes were identified by the PADLOC and DefenseFinder tools, except for AbiU and RM type II which were only identified by PADLOC. Most bacteria, including <span class="html-italic">Salmonella,</span> possess multiple antiviral defence systems that protect against infection by phages and mobile genetic elements [<a href="#B47-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">47</a>].</div><div class="html-p">Seven prophages were detected in the <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 genome, two were intact, and five were incomplete. By comparison, in <span class="html-italic">S. enterica</span> Typhimurium ms202 nine prophages were detected, two were intact, five were incomplete and two were questionable [<a href="#B63-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">63</a>]. Moreover, <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 had not only <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> prophage sequences (e.g. phage RE-2010) but also prophages annotated as belonging to closely related genera <span class="html-italic">Shigella</span> (phage POCJ13) and <span class="html-italic">Escherichia</span> (phage 500465-2), which may indicate horizontal gene transfer or polyvalent phages. A previous study has reported that phage populations in <span class="html-italic">S. enterica</span> contribute to horizontal gene transfer, including virulence and virulence-related genes within the subspecies [<a href="#B76-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">76</a>,<a href="#B77-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">77</a>,<a href="#B78-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">78</a>,<a href="#B79-microorganisms-11-00111" class="html-bibr">79</a>]. Further studies on <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> phages may uncover the receptor-interaction mechanisms between phages and hosts which may lead to improving phage therapy as an option for the treatment or control of <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span>.</div></section><section id="sec5-microorganisms-11-00111" type="conclusions"><h2 data-nested="1">  5. Conclusions</h2><div class="html-p">Salmonellosis is a healthcare issue around the world, so genomic analysis of <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> isolates could be a key determinant for better control of salmonellosis. Our study showed the effectiveness of a hybrid sequence assembly approach for environmental <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> genome analysis using HiSeq and MinION data. <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 was determined to belong to a monophyletic clade containing 23 <span class="html-italic">S. enterica</span> strains of serovar Enteritidis. The hybrid genome assembly enabled mobile genetic elements, genomic islands, <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> Pathogenicity Islands, antiviral systems, antimicrobial resistance genes, virulence genes, and prophages to be identified in <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3. Furthermore, a gene encoding heavy metal resistance, arsC, was detected. These data are important to inform the control of <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> and heavy metal pollution in the Santo Amaro region of Brazil. </div></section>
  </div>

      Genome similarity of <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3. (<b>A</b>) Distance tree of <span class="html-italic">Salmonella enterica</span> built using a combined matrix of all Mash and fastANI pairwise distances of <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 and 1598 genomes. Genomes classified by GTBD as <span class="html-italic">S. enterica</span> are shaded in blue. (<b>B</b>) Mash-distance values of <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 were calculated with 1598 <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> genomes. The maximum Mash-distance threshold (0.03) used to select genomes is represented by a dotted line.

  <div class="html-fig_description">
    <b>Figure 3.</b>
      Pangenome of <span class="html-italic">Salmonella enterica</span> and phylogeny of <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3. (<b>A</b>) Gene frequency of <span class="html-italic">S. enterica</span> pangenome. (<b>B</b>) Number of gene families in the <span class="html-italic">S. enterica</span> pangenome. The cumulative curve (in red) and an alpha value of the Heaps Law less than one (0.52) supports an open pangenome. (<b>C</b>) core-genome SNP tree of <span class="html-italic">Salmonella enterica</span> highlighting the phylogenetic group contained the <span class="html-italic">Salmonella</span> SE3 genome. The monophyletic clade containing the serovar Enteritidis of <span class="html-italic">S. enterica</span> is shaded in cool grey. Bootstrap values below and above 70% are represented by blue and dark-grey dots, respectively.

        (2023). Hybrid Genomic Analysis of <i>Salmonella enterica</i> Serovar Enteritidis SE3 Isolated from Polluted Soil in Brazil. <em>Microorganisms</em>, <em>11</em>(1), 111.

            // old browser fix - this way the console log rows won't throw (silent) errors in browsers not supporting console log
            if (!window.console) window.console = {};
            if (!window.console.log) window.console.log = function () { };

            var currentJournalNameSystem = "microorganisms";

            $(document).ready(function()
            {
                $('select.foundation-select').multiselect({
                    search: true,
                    minHeight: 130,
                    maxHeight: 130,
                });

                $(document).foundation({
                    orbit: {
                        timer_speed: 4000,
                    },
                    reveal: {
                        animation: 'fadeAndPop',
                        animation_speed: 100,
                    }
                });

                $(".chosen-select").each(function(element) {
                    var maxSelected = (undefined !== $(this).data('maxselectedoptions') ? $(this).data('maxselectedoptions') : 100);

                    $(this).on('chosen:ready', function(event, data) {
                        var select = $(data.chosen.form_field);
                        if (select.attr('id') === 'journal-browser-volume') {
                            $(data.chosen.dropdown).addClass('UI_JournalBrowser_Volume_Options');
                        }
                        if (select.attr('id') === 'journal-browser-issue') {
                            $(data.chosen.dropdown).addClass('UI_JournalBrowser_Issue_Options');
                        }
                    }).chosen({ display_disabled_options: false, disable_search_threshold: 7, max_selected_options: maxSelected, width: "100%" });
                });

                $(".toEncode").each(function(e) {
                    var oldHref = $(this).attr("href");
                    var newHref = oldHref.replace('.botdefense.please.enable.javascript.','@');
                    $(this).attr("href", newHref);

                    if (!$(this).hasClass("emailCaptcha"))
                    {
                        $(this).html(newHref.replace('mailto:', ''));
                    }
                    
                    
                    $(this).removeClass("visibility-hidden");
                });

                $(document).on('opened.fndtn.reveal', '[data-reveal]', function()
                {
                    $(document).foundation('equalizer', 'reflow');
                });

                // fix the images that have tag height / width defined
                // otherwise the default foundation styles overwrite the tag definitions
                $("img").each(function()
                {
                   if ($(this).attr('width') != undefined || $(this).attr('height') != undefined)
                   {
                       $(this).addClass("img-fixed");
                   }
                });

                $("#basic_search, #advanced_search").submit(function(e) {
                    var searchArguments = false;

                    $(this).find("input,select").not("#search,.search-button").each(function() {
                        if (undefined === $(this).val() || "" === $(this).val()) {
                            $(this).attr('name', null);
                        } else {
                            $(this).attr('name');
                            searchArguments = true;
                        }
                    });

                    if (!searchArguments) {
                        window.location = $(this).attr('action');
                        return false;
                    }
                });

                $(".hide-show-desktop-option").click(function(e) 
                {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var parentDiv = $(this).closest("div");

                    $.ajax({
                        url: $(this).attr('href'),
                        success: function(msg)
                        {
                            parentDiv.removeClass().hide();
                        }
                    });
                });

                $(".generic-toggleable-header").click(function(e) {
                    $(this).toggleClass("active");
                    $(this).next(".generic-toggleable-content").toggleClass("active");
                });

                /*
                * handle whole row as a link if the row contains only one visible link
                */
                $("table.new tr").hover(function()
                {
                   if ($(this).find("td:visible a").length == 1)
                   {
                       $(this).addClass("single-link");
                   }
                },
                function()
                {
                   $(this).removeClass("single-link");
                });

                $("table.new:not(.table-of-tables)").on("click", "tr.single-link", function(e)
                {
                    var target = $(e.target);
                    if (!e.ctrlKey && !target.is("a")) {
                        $(this).find("td:visible a")[0].click();
                    }
                });

                $(document).on("click", ".custom-accordion-for-small-screen-link", function(e) {
                    if ($(this).closest("#basic_search").length > 0) {
                        if ($(".search-container__advanced").first().is(":visible")) {
                            openAdvanced()
                        }
                    }

                    if (Foundation.utils.is_small_only()) {
                        if ($(this).hasClass("active")) {
                            $(this).removeClass("active");
                            $(this).next(".custom-accordion-for-small-screen-content").addClass("show-for-medium-up");
                        }
                        else {
                            $(this).addClass("active");
                            $(this).next(".custom-accordion-for-small-screen-content").removeClass("show-for-medium-up");
                            $(document).foundation('orbit', 'reflow');
                        }
                    }

                    if (undefined !== $(this).data("callback")) {
                        var customCallback = $(this).data("callback");
                        func = window[customCallback];
                        func();
                    }
                });

                $(document).on("click", ".js-open-small-search", function(e) {
                    e.preventDefault();  
                    $(this).toggleClass("active").closest(".tab-bar").toggleClass("active");
                    $(".search-container").toggleClass("hide-for-small-down");
                });

                $(document).on("click", ".js-open-menu", function(e) {
                    $(".search-container").addClass("hide-for-small-down");

                });

                $(window).on('resize', function() {
                    recalculate_main_browser_position();
                    recalculate_responsive_moving_containers();
                });

                                updateSearchLabelVisibilities();
                recalculate_main_browser_position();
                recalculate_responsive_moving_containers();
                
                
                if (window.document.documentMode == 11) {
                    $("<link/>", { rel: "stylesheet", type: "text/css", href: "https://fonts.googleapis.com/icon?family=Material+Icons"}).appendTo("head");
                }
            });

            function recalculate_main_browser_position() {
                if (Foundation.utils.is_small_only()) {
                    if ($("#js-main-top-container").parent("#js-large-main-top-container").length > 0) {
                        $("#js-main-top-container").appendTo($("#js-small-main-top-container"));
                    }
                }
                else {
                    if ($("#js-main-top-container").parent("#js-small-main-top-container").length > 0) {
                        $("#js-main-top-container").appendTo($("#js-large-main-top-container"));
                    }
                }
            }

            function recalculate_responsive_moving_containers() {
                $(".responsive-moving-container.large").each(function() {
                    var previousParent = $(".responsive-moving-container.active[data-id='"+$(this).data("id")+"']");
                    var movingContent  = previousParent.html();

                    if (Foundation.utils.is_small_only()) {
                        var currentParent = $(".responsive-moving-container.small[data-id='"+$(this).data("id")+"']");
                    }
                    else if (Foundation.utils.is_medium_only()) {
                        var currentParent = $(".responsive-moving-container.medium[data-id='"+$(this).data("id")+"']");
                    }
                    else {
                        var currentParent = $(".responsive-moving-container.large[data-id='"+$(this).data("id")+"']");
                    }

                    if (previousParent.attr("class") !== currentParent.attr("class")) {
                        currentParent.html(movingContent);
                        previousParent.html();
                        currentParent.addClass("active");
                        previousParent.removeClass("active");
                    }
                });
            }

            // cookies allowed is checked from a) local storage and b) from server separately so that the footer bar doesn't
            // get included in the custom page caches
            function checkCookiesAllowed() {
                var cookiesEnabled = localStorage.getItem("mdpi_cookies_enabled"); 

                if (null === cookiesEnabled) {
                    $.ajax({
                        url: "/ajax_cookie_value/mdpi_cookies_accepted",
                        success: function(data)
                        {
                            if (data.value) {
                                localStorage.setItem("mdpi_cookies_enabled", true);
                                checkDisplaySurvey();
                            }
                            else {
                                $(".js-allow-cookies").show();
                            }
                        }
                    });
                }
                else {
                    checkDisplaySurvey();
                }
            }

            function checkDisplaySurvey() {
                            }

                        window.addEventListener('CookiebotOnAccept', function (e) {
                var CookieDate = new Date;

                if (Cookiebot.consent.preferences) {
                    CookieDate.setFullYear(CookieDate.getFullYear() + 1);
                    document.cookie = "mdpi_layout_type_v2=mobile; path=/; expires=" + CookieDate.toUTCString() + ";";
                    $(".js-toggle-desktop-layout-link").css("display", "inline-block");
                }
            }, false);

            window.addEventListener('CookiebotOnDecline', function (e) {
                if (!Cookiebot.consent.preferences) {
                    $(".js-toggle-desktop-layout-link").hide();

                    if ("" === "desktop") {
                        window.location = "/toggle_desktop_layout_cookie";
                    }
                }
            }, false);
            
            var hash = $(location).attr('hash');
            if ("#share" === hash) {
                if (1 === $("#main-share-modal").length) {
                    $('#main-share-modal').foundation('reveal', 'open');
                }
            }

    <script type="text/javascript">
        $(document).ready(function()
        {
            var helpFunctions = $(".middle-column__help__fixed");
            var leftColumnAffix = $(".left-column__fixed");
            var middleColumn  = $("#middle-column");
            var clone = null;

            helpFunctions.affix({
                offset: {
                    top: function() {
                        return middleColumn.offset().top - 8 - (Foundation.utils.is_medium_only() ? 30 : 0);
                    },
                    bottom: function() {
                        return $("#footer").innerHeight() + 74 + (Foundation.utils.is_medium_only() ? 0 : 0);
                    }
                }
            });

            if (leftColumnAffix.length > 0) {
                clone = leftColumnAffix.clone();
                clone.addClass("left-column__fixed__affix");
                clone.insertBefore(leftColumnAffix);
                clone.css('width', leftColumnAffix.outerWidth() + 50);

                clone.affix({
                    offset: {
                        top: function() {
                            return leftColumnAffix.offset().top - 30 - (Foundation.utils.is_medium_only() ? 50 : 0);
                        },
                        bottom: function() {
                            return $("#footer").innerHeight() + 92 + (Foundation.utils.is_medium_only() ? 0 : 0);
                        }
                    }
                });
            }

            $(window).on("resize", function() {
                if (clone !== null) {
                    clone.css('width', leftColumnAffix.outerWidth() + 50);
                }
            });

            new ClipboardJS('.js-clipboard-copy');
        });
    </script>

    <script>
        $(document).ready(function()
        {
            
            $(".link-article-menu").click(function(e) {
                e.preventDefault();

                $(this).find('span').toggle();
                $(this).next("div").toggleClass("active");
            });

            
            $(".js-similarity-related-articles").click(function(e) {
                e.preventDefault();

                if ('' !== $('#recommended-articles-modal').attr('data-url')) {
                    $('#recommended-articles-modal').foundation('reveal', 'open', $('#recommended-articles-modal').attr('data-url'));
                }
            });

                        $.ajax({
                url: "/article/1021937/similarity-related/show-link",
                success: function(result) {
                    if (result.show) {
                        $('#recommended-articles-modal').attr('data-url', result.link);
                        $('.js-article-similarity-container').show();
                    }
                }
            });
            
            $(document).on('opened.fndtn.reveal', '[data-reveal]', function()
            {
                var modal = $(this);
                if (modal.attr('id') === "author-biographies-modal") {
                    modal.find('.multiple-items').slick({
                        slidesToShow: 1,
                        nextArrow: '<a class="slick-next" href="#"><i class="material-icons">chevron_right</i></a>',
                        prevArrow: '<a class="slick-prev" href="#"><i class="material-icons">chevron_left</i></a>',
                        slidesToScroll: 1,
                        draggable: false,
                    });

                    modal.find('.multiple-items').slick('refresh');
                }
            });
        });
    </script>

                        document.querySelector("hypothesis-adder").shadowRoot.querySelector(".annotator-adder").style.visibility = "hidden";
                    }

                }
            });
